<p><b>Agriculture</b> is the cultivation and breeding of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> for <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiber" title="Fiber">fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_plants" title="Medicinal plants">medicinal plants</a> and other products used to sustain and enhance human life.<sup id="cite_ref-Office1999_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Office1999-1">[1]</a></sup> Agriculture was the key development in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Human civilization">human civilization</a>, whereby farming of <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> species created food <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">surpluses</a> that nurtured the development of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>. The study of agriculture is known as <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">agricultural science</a>. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">history of agriculture</a> dates back thousands of years, and its development has been driven and defined by greatly different <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>, and technologies. <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial agriculture">Industrial agriculture</a> based on large-scale <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> farming has become the dominant agricultural methodology.</p>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">agronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agrochemical" title="Agrochemical">agrochemicals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, and technological developments have in many cases sharply increased yields from cultivation, but at the same time have caused widespread ecological damage and negative human health effects. <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">Selective breeding</a> and modern practices in <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> have similarly increased the output of meat, but have raised concerns about <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> and the health effects of the <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a>, and other chemicals commonly used in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Industrial_meat_production&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Industrial meat production (page does not exist)">industrial meat production</a>. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organisms</a> are an increasing component of agriculture, although they are banned in several countries. Agricultural food production and water management are increasingly becoming global issues that are fostering debate on a number of fronts. Significant degradation of land and water resources, including the depletion of <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, has been observed in recent decades, and the effects of global warming on agriculture and of agriculture on global warming are still not fully understood.</p>
<p>The major agricultural products can be broadly grouped into foods, fibers, <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw materials</a>. Specific foods include <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> (grains), <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">oils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a>. Fibers include <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>. Raw materials include <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a>. Other useful materials are also produced by plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_dye" title="Natural dye">dyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perfume" title="Perfume">perfumes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> and ornamental products such as <a href="/wiki/Floriculture" title="Floriculture">cut flowers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nursery_(horticulture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nursery (horticulture)">nursery plants</a>. Over one third of the world's workers are employed in agriculture, second only to the service sector, although the percentages of agricultural workers in developed countries has decreased significantly over the past several centuries.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_terminology">Etymology and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>agriculture</i> is a late <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> adaptation of Latin <i>agricultūra</i>, from <i>ager</i>, "field", and <i>cultūra</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivation</a>" or "growing".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Agriculture usually refers to human activities, although it is also observed in certain species of <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambrosia_beetle" title="Ambrosia beetle">ambrosia beetle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> To practice agriculture means to use natural resources to "produce commodities which maintain life, including food, fiber, forest products, horticultural crops, and their related services."<sup id="cite_ref-Maine_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maine-4">[4]</a></sup> This definition includes <a href="/wiki/Arable_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Arable farming">arable farming</a> or <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">agronomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a>, all terms for the growing of plants, <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maine_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maine-4">[4]</a></sup> A distinction is sometimes made between forestry and agriculture, based on the former's longer management rotations, extensive versus intensive management practices and development mainly by nature, rather than by man. Even then, it is acknowledged that there is a large amount of knowledge transfer and overlap between <a href="/wiki/Silviculture" title="Silviculture">silviculture</a> (the management of forests) and agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In traditional farming, the two are often combined even on small landholdings, leading to the term <a href="/wiki/Agroforestry" title="Agroforestry">agroforestry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ClaySumerianSickle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/310px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg" width="310" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/465px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/620px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ClaySumerianSickle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> harvester's <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickle</a> made from baked clay (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3000</span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BC)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Agriculture began independently in different parts of the globe, and included a diverse range of taxa. At least 11 separate regions of the Old and New World were involved as independent <a href="/wiki/Centers_of_origin" class="mw-redirect" title="Centers of origin">centers of origin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Wild grains were collected and eaten from at least 105,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">Pigs</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia around 15,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> was domesticated in China between 13,500 and 8,200 years ago, followed by <a href="/wiki/Mung_bean" title="Mung bean">mung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy" class="mw-redirect" title="Soy">soy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azuki_bean" class="mw-redirect" title="Azuki bean">azuki</a> beans. <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia between 13,000 and 11,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> From around 11,500 years ago, the eight <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_founder_crops" title="Neolithic founder crops">Neolithic founder crops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmer_wheat" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmer wheat">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn wheat</a>, hulled <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicia_ervilia" title="Vicia ervilia">bitter vetch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chick_pea" class="mw-redirect" title="Chick pea">chick peas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> were cultivated in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated from the wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and Pakistan some 10,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-McTavish_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-11">[11]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> of South America, the <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> was domesticated between 10,000 and 7,000 years ago, along with beans, <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a> and some <a href="/wiki/List_of_root_vegetables" title="List of root vegetables">root vegetables</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> around 9,000 years ago. <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a> was domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region of Africa by 7,000 years ago. <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 5,600 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-Broudy1979_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broudy1979-12">[12]</a></sup> and was independently domesticated in Eurasia at an unknown time. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">In Mesoamerica</a>, wild <a href="/wiki/Teosinte" class="mw-redirect" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> was domesticated to <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> by 6,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Middle Ages, both <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">in the Islamic world</a> and in Europe, agriculture was transformed with improved techniques and the diffusion of crop plants, including the introduction of sugar, rice, cotton and fruit trees such as the <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a> to Europe by way of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatGeographic2015_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeographic2015-15">[15]</a></sup> After 1492, the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a> brought New World crops such as maize, potatoes, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manioc" class="mw-redirect" title="Manioc">manioc</a> to Europe, and Old World crops such as wheat, barley, rice and <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a>, and livestock including horses, cattle, sheep and goats to the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> were introduced soon after the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and developed much further in the past 200 years, starting with the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a>. Since 1900, agriculture in the developed nations, and to a lesser extent in the developing world, has seen large rises in productivity as human labor has been replaced by <a href="/wiki/Mechanized_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mechanized farming">mechanization</a>, and assisted by <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fertilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Synthetic fertilizer">synthetic fertilizers</a>, pesticides, and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Haber-Bosch" class="mw-redirect" title="Haber-Bosch">Haber-Bosch</a> method allowed the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> fertilizer on an industrial scale, greatly increasing <a href="/wiki/Crop_yields" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop yields">crop yields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Modern agriculture has raised political issues including <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">farm subsidies</a>, leading to alternative approaches such as the <a href="/wiki/Organic_movement" title="Organic movement">organic movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-motherjones1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-motherjones1-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-smh.com.au_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smh.com.au-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_civilization">Agriculture and civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Agriculture and civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Plants_in_culture" title="Plants in culture">Plants in culture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">Civilization</a> was the product of the Agricultural <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>; as <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> put it, "civilization was the agricultural surplus."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In the course of history, civilization coincided in space with fertile areas such as <a href="/wiki/The_Fertile_Crescent" class="mw-redirect" title="The Fertile Crescent">The Fertile Crescent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carneiro%27s_circumscription_theory" title="Carneiro's circumscription theory">states formed mainly in circumscribed agricultural lands</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Wall">Great Wall</a> of China and the Roman empire's <i><a href="/wiki/Limes" title="Limes">limes</a></i> (borders) demarcated the same northern frontier of cereal agriculture. This cereal belt fed the civilizations formed in the <a href="/wiki/Axial_Age" title="Axial Age">Axial Age</a> and connected by the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Ancient Egyptians, whose agriculture depended exclusively on the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, deified the river, worshipped, and exalted it in a great hymn.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The Chinese imperial court issued numerous edicts, stating: "Agriculture is the foundation of this Empire."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Egyptian, Mesopotamian, Chinese, and Inca Emperors themselves plowed ceremonial fields in order to show personal example to everyone.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ancient strategists, Chinese <a href="/wiki/Guan_Zhong" title="Guan Zhong">Guan Zhong</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Shang_Yang" title="Shang Yang">Shang Yang</a><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and Indian <a href="/wiki/Kautilya" class="mw-redirect" title="Kautilya">Kautilya</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> drew doctrines linking agriculture with military power. Agriculture defined the limits on how large and for how long an army could be mobilized. Shang Yang called agriculture and war the <i>One</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the vast human pantheon of agricultural deities<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> there are several deities who combined the functions of agriculture and war.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the Neolithic Agricultural Revolution produced civilization, the modern Agricultural Revolution, begun in Britain (<a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a>), made possible the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_civilization" title="Industrial civilization">industrial civilization</a>. The first precondition for industry was greater yields by less manpower, resulting in greater percentage of manpower available for non-agricultural sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_agriculture">Types of agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Types of agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/220px-Reindeer_herding.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/330px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/440px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">Reindeer</a> herds form the basis of pastoral agriculture for several Arctic and Subarctic peoples.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">Pastoralism</a> involves managing domesticated animals. In <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_pastoralism" title="Nomadic pastoralism">nomadic pastoralism</a>, herds of livestock are moved from place to place in search of pasture, fodder, and water. This type of farming is practised in arid and semi-arid regions of Sahara, Central Asia and some parts of India.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivation</a>, a small area of a forest is cleared by cutting down all the trees and the area is burned. The land is then used for growing crops for several years. When the soil becomes less fertile, the area is then abandoned. Another patch of land is selected and the process is repeated. This type of farming is practiced mainly in areas with abundant rainfall where the forest regenerates quickly. This practice is used in Northeast India, Southeast Asia, and the Amazon Basin.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsistence farming">Subsistence farming</a> is practiced to satisfy family or local needs alone, with little left over for transport elsewhere. It is intensively practiced in Monsoon Asia and South-East Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a>, the crops are cultivated for commercial purpose i.e., for selling. The main motive of the farmer is to make profit, with a low fallow ratio and a high use of inputs. This type of farming is mainly practiced in highly developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcFactSheet_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcFactSheet-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_agriculture">Contemporary agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contemporary agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg/220px-Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg/330px-Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg/440px-Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Precision_Farming_in_Minnesota_-_Natural_Colour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">Satellite</a> image of farming in <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><br />
In the past century, agriculture has been characterized by increased productivity, the substitution of synthetic fertilizers and pesticides for labor, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">farm subsidies</a>. In recent years there has been a backlash against the <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">external</a> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_awareness" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental awareness">environmental effects</a> of conventional agriculture, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a> movements.<sup id="cite_ref-motherjones1_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-motherjones1-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> One of the major forces behind this movement has been the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, which first certified <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic food</a> in 1991 and began reform of its <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a> (CAP) in 2005 to phase out commodity-linked farm subsidies,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> also known as <a href="/wiki/Decoupling_and_re-coupling" title="Decoupling and re-coupling">decoupling</a>. The growth of organic farming has renewed research in alternative technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a> and selective breeding. Recent mainstream technological developments include <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a>.</p>
<p>In 2007, higher incentives for farmers to grow non-food biofuel crops<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> combined with other factors, such as over development of former farm lands, rising transportation costs, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, growing consumer demand in China and India, and <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> caused <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food shortages</a> in Asia, the Middle East, Africa, and Mexico, as well as rising food prices around the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> As of December 2007, 37 countries faced food crises, and 20 had imposed some sort of food-price controls. Some of these shortages resulted in <a href="/wiki/2007-2008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007-2008 world food price crisis">food riots</a> and even deadly stampedes.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timesonline.co.uk_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline.co.uk-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-45">[45]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">International Fund for Agricultural Development</a> posits that an increase in <a href="/wiki/Smallholding" title="Smallholding">smallholder agriculture</a> may be part of the solution to concerns about food prices and overall food security. They in part base this on the experience of Vietnam, which went from a food importer to large food exporter and saw a significant drop in poverty, due mainly to the development of smallholder agriculture in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Disease and land degradation are two of the major concerns in agriculture today. For example, an epidemic of <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">stem rust</a> on wheat caused by the <a href="/wiki/Ug99" title="Ug99">Ug99</a> lineage is currently spreading across Africa and into Asia and is causing major concerns due to crop losses of 70% or more under some conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Approximately 40% of the world's agricultural land is seriously degraded.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In Africa, if current trends of soil degradation continue, the continent might be able to feed just 25% of its population by 2025, according to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_University" title="United Nations University">United Nations University's</a> Ghana-based Institute for Natural Resources in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Agrarian structure is a long-term structure in the <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Braudel" title="Fernand Braudel">Braudelian</a> understanding of the concept. On a larger scale the agrarian structure is more dependent on the regional, social, cultural and historical factors than on the state’s undertaken activities. Like in Poland, where despite running an intense agrarian policy for many years, the agrarian structure in 2002 has much in common with that found in 1921 soon after the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1795%E2%80%931918)" title="History of Poland (1795–1918)">partitions period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">agricultural output of China</a> was the largest in the world, followed by the European Union, India and the United States, according to the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Agriculture#List_of_countries_by_agricultural_output" title="Agriculture">see below</a></i>). Economists measure the <a href="/wiki/Total_factor_productivity" title="Total factor productivity">total factor productivity</a> of agriculture and by this measure agriculture in the United States is roughly 1.7 times more productive than it was in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Workforce">Workforce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Workforce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a> states that approximately one billion people, or over 1/3 of the available work force, are employed in the global agricultural sector. Agriculture constitutes approximately 70% of the global employment of children, and in many countries employs the largest percentage of women of any industry.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Service_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Service sector">service sector</a> only overtook the agricultural sector as the largest global employer in 2007. Between 1997 and 2007, the percentage of people employed in agriculture fell by over four percentage points, a trend that is expected to continue.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The number of people employed in agriculture varies widely on a per-country basis, ranging from less than 2% in countries like the US and Canada to over 80% in many African nations.<sup id="cite_ref-LaborForce_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaborForce-54">[54]</a></sup> In developed countries, these figures are significantly lower than in previous centuries. During the 16th century in Europe, for example, between 55 and 75 percent of the population was engaged in agriculture, depending on the country. By the 19th century in Europe, this had dropped to between 35 and 65 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In the same countries today, the figure is less than 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-LaborForce_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaborForce-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/220px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/330px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/440px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rollover_protection_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Rollover protection system">Rollover protection bar</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Fordson_tractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fordson tractor">Fordson tractor</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_safety_and_health" title="Agricultural safety and health">Agricultural safety and health</a></div>
<p>Agriculture, specifically <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">farming</a>, remains a hazardous industry, and farmers worldwide remain at high risk of work-related injuries, lung disease, <a href="/wiki/Noise-induced_hearing_loss" title="Noise-induced hearing loss">noise-induced hearing loss</a>, skin diseases, as well as certain cancers related to chemical use and prolonged sun exposure. On <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial agriculture">industrialized farms</a>, injuries frequently involve the use of <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_machinery" title="Agricultural machinery">agricultural machinery</a>, and a common cause of fatal agricultural injuries in developed countries is tractor rollovers.<sup id="cite_ref-aginjury_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aginjury-56">[56]</a></sup> Pesticides and other chemicals used in farming can also be hazardous to worker health, and workers exposed to pesticides may experience illness or have children with birth defects.<sup id="cite_ref-NIOSH_pest_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_pest-57">[57]</a></sup> As an industry in which families commonly share in work and live on the farm itself, entire families can be at risk for injuries, illness, and death.<sup id="cite_ref-NIOSH_Agri_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_Agri-58">[58]</a></sup> Common causes of fatal injuries among young farm workers include drowning, machinery and motor vehicle-related accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-NIOSH_Agri_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_Agri-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The International Labour Organization considers agriculture "one of the most hazardous of all economic sectors."<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-52">[52]</a></sup> It estimates that the annual work-related death toll among agricultural employees is at least 170,000, twice the average rate of other jobs. In addition, incidences of death, injury and illness related to agricultural activities often go unreported.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO2_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO2-59">[59]</a></sup> The organization has developed the <a href="/wiki/Safety_and_Health_in_Agriculture_Convention,_2001" title="Safety and Health in Agriculture Convention, 2001">Safety and Health in Agriculture Convention, 2001</a>, which covers the range of risks in the agriculture occupation, the prevention of these risks and the role that individuals and organizations engaged in agriculture should play.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_production_systems">Agricultural production systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Agricultural production systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_cultivation_systems">Crop cultivation systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Crop cultivation systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg/290px-Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg" width="290" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg/435px-Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg/580px-Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1963" data-file-height="679" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paddy_fields_in_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rice cultivation in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, India</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cropping systems vary among farms depending on the available resources and constraints; geography and climate of the farm; government policy; economic, social and political pressures; and the philosophy and culture of the farmer.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_FS_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_FS-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PCP_APS_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_APS-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">Shifting cultivation</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" class="mw-redirect" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a>) is a system in which forests are burnt, releasing nutrients to support cultivation of annual and then <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> crops for a period of several years.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup> Then the plot is left fallow to regrow forest, and the farmer moves to a new plot, returning after many more years (10 – 20). This fallow period is shortened if population density grows, requiring the input of nutrients (fertilizer or <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>) and some manual <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a>. Annual cultivation is the next phase of intensity in which there is no fallow period. This requires even greater nutrient and pest control inputs.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Banaue_Rice_Terraces_(Viewpoint).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg/220px-Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg/330px-Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg/440px-Banaue_Rice_Terraces_%28Viewpoint%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="496" data-file-height="379" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Banaue_Rice_Terraces_(Viewpoint).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Banaue_Rice_Terraces" title="Banaue Rice Terraces">Banaue Rice Terraces</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ifugao" title="Ifugao">Ifugao</a>, Philippines</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Further industrialization led to the use of <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monocultures</a>, when one <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> is planted on a large acreage. Because of the low <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, nutrient use is uniform and pests tend to build up, necessitating the greater use of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and fertilizers.<sup id="cite_ref-PCP_APS_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_APS-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Multiple_cropping" title="Multiple cropping">Multiple cropping</a>, in which several crops are grown sequentially in one year, and <a href="/wiki/Intercropping" title="Intercropping">intercropping</a>, when several crops are grown at the same time, are other kinds of annual cropping systems known as <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polycultures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> environments, the timing and extent of agriculture may be limited by rainfall, either not allowing multiple annual crops in a year, or requiring <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>. In all of these environments perennial crops are grown (coffee, chocolate) and systems are practiced such as agroforestry. In <a href="/wiki/Temperateness" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperateness">temperate</a> environments, where ecosystems were predominantly <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> or <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairie</a>, highly productive annual farming is the dominant agricultural system.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_statistics">Crop statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Crop statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_important_agricultural_crops_worldwide" class="mw-redirect" title="List of most important agricultural crops worldwide">List of most important agricultural crops worldwide</a></div>
<p>Important categories of crops include <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudocereals" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudocereals">pseudocereals</a>, pulses (legumes), forage, and fruits and vegetables. Specific crops are cultivated in distinct <a href="/wiki/Growing_region" title="Growing region">growing regions</a> throughout the world. In millions of metric tons, based on <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">FAO</a> estimate.</p>
