<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_production_systems">Livestock production systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Livestock production systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">Livestock</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals">List of domesticated animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KerbauJawa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/KerbauJawa.jpg/220px-KerbauJawa.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/KerbauJawa.jpg/330px-KerbauJawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/KerbauJawa.jpg/440px-KerbauJawa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="991" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KerbauJawa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ploughing rice <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddy fields</a> with <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Animals, including horses, <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">mules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">oxen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a>, and dogs, are often used to help cultivate fields, <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvest</a> crops, wrangle other animals, and transport farm products to buyers. Animal husbandry not only refers to the breeding and raising of animals for meat or to harvest animal products (like milk, <a href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>) on a continual basis, but also to the breeding and care of species for work and companionship.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/220px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/330px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/440px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An ox-pulled plough in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Livestock production systems can be defined based on feed source, as grassland-based, mixed, and landless.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-64">[64]</a></sup> As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 30% of Earth's ice- and water-free area was used for producing livestock, with the sector employing approximately 1.3 billion people. Between the 1960s and the 2000s, there was a significant increase in livestock production, both by numbers and by carcass weight, especially among beef, pigs and chickens, the latter of which had production increased by almost a factor of 10. Non-meat animals, such as milk cows and egg-producing chickens, also showed significant production increases. Global cattle, sheep and goat populations are expected to continue to increase sharply through 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-LP_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a> or fish farming, the production of fish for human consumption in confined operations, is one of the fastest growing sectors of food production, growing at an average of 9% a year between 1975 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the second half of the 20th century, producers using <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> focused on creating livestock <a href="/wiki/Breed" title="Breed">breeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crossbreed" title="Crossbreed">crossbreeds</a> that increased production, while mostly disregarding the need to preserve <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>. This trend has led to a significant decrease in genetic diversity and resources among livestock breeds, leading to a corresponding decrease in disease resistance and local adaptations previously found among traditional breeds.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grassland based livestock production relies upon plant material such as <a href="/wiki/Shrubland" title="Shrubland">shrubland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangeland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Managed_intensive_rotational_grazing" title="Managed intensive rotational grazing">pastures</a> for feeding <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a> animals. Outside nutrient inputs may be used, however manure is returned directly to the grassland as a major nutrient source. This system is particularly important in areas where crop production is not feasible because of climate or soil, representing 30 – 40 million pastoralists.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup> Mixed production systems use grassland, <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> crops and grain feed crops as feed for ruminant and monogastric (one stomach; mainly chickens and pigs) livestock. Manure is typically recycled in mixed systems as a fertilizer for crops.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Landless systems rely upon feed from outside the farm, representing the de-linking of crop and livestock production found more prevalently in <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a>(OECD) member countries. Synthetic fertilizers are more heavily relied upon for crop production and manure utilization becomes a challenge as well as a source for pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-64">[64]</a></sup> Industrialized countries use these operations to produce much of the global supplies of poultry and pork. Scientists estimate that 75% of the growth in livestock production between 2003 and 2030 will be in <a href="/wiki/Confined_animal_feeding_operations" class="mw-redirect" title="Confined animal feeding operations">confined animal feeding operations</a>, sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Factory_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Factory farming">factory farming</a>. Much of this growth is happening in developing countries in Asia, with much smaller amounts of growth in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-LP_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-65">[65]</a></sup> Some of the practices used in commercial livestock production, including the usage of <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a>, are controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_practices">Production practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg/220px-Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg/330px-Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg/440px-Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2567" data-file-height="1299" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mt_Uluguru_and_Sisal_plantations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Road leading across the farm allows machinery access to the farm for production practices</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Farming is the practice of agriculture by specialized labor in an area primarily devoted to agricultural processes, in service of a dislocated population usually in a <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">city</a>.</p>
<p>Tillage is the practice of plowing soil to prepare for planting or for nutrient incorporation or for pest control. Tillage varies in intensity from conventional to <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">no-till</a>. It may improve productivity by warming the soil, incorporating fertilizer and controlling weeds, but also renders soil more prone to erosion, triggers the decomposition of organic matter releasing CO<sub>2</sub>, and reduces the abundance and diversity of soil organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Soil_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soil-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PCP_Tillage_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Tillage-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pest control includes the management of weeds, <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, and diseases. Chemical (pesticides), biological (<a href="/wiki/Biocontrol" class="mw-redirect" title="Biocontrol">biocontrol</a>), mechanical (tillage), and cultural practices are used. Cultural practices include <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culling" title="Culling">culling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover crops</a>, intercropping, <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">composting</a>, avoidance, and <a href="/wiki/Disease_resistance_in_fruit_and_vegetables" title="Disease resistance in fruit and vegetables">resistance</a>. Integrated pest management attempts to use all of these methods to keep pest populations below the number which would cause economic loss, and recommends pesticides as a last resort.<sup id="cite_ref-PCP_Pest_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Pest-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nutrient_management" title="Nutrient management">Nutrient management</a> includes both the source of nutrient inputs for crop and livestock production, and the method of utilization of <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> produced by livestock. Nutrient inputs can be chemical inorganic fertilizers, <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manure</a>, compost and mined <a href="/wiki/Minerals" class="mw-redirect" title="Minerals">minerals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PCP_Soil_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Soil-72">[72]</a></sup> Crop nutrient use may also be managed using cultural techniques such as crop rotation or a <a href="/wiki/Fallow" class="mw-redirect" title="Fallow">fallow</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_nutrient_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS_nutrient-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Soil_nutrient_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soil_nutrient-74">[74]</a></sup> Manure is used either by holding livestock where the feed crop is growing, such as in managed intensive rotational grazing, or <a href="/wiki/Manure_spreader" title="Manure spreader">by spreading</a> either dry or liquid formulations of manure on cropland or <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Water management">Water management</a> is needed where rainfall is insufficient or variable, which occurs to some degree in most regions of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup> Some farmers use <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> to supplement rainfall. In other areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> in the U.S. and Canada, farmers use a fallow year to conserve soil moisture to use for growing a crop in the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-PCP_Water_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Water-75">[75]</a></sup> Agriculture represents 70% of freshwater use worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimentel_water_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimentel_water-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a report by the <a href="/wiki/International_Food_Policy_Research_Institute" title="International Food Policy Research Institute">International Food Policy Research Institute</a>, agricultural technologies will have the greatest impact on food production if adopted in combination with each other; using a model that assessed how eleven technologies could impact agricultural productivity, food security and trade by 2050, the International Food Policy Research Institute found that the number of people at risk from hunger could be reduced by as much as 40% and food prices could be reduced by almost half.<sup id="cite_ref-ifpri_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ifpri-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Payment_for_ecosystem_services" title="Payment for ecosystem services">Payment for ecosystem services</a> (PES) can further incentivise efforts to green the agriculture sector. This is an approach that verifies values and rewards the benefits of ecosystem services provided by green agricultural practices."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup> "Innovative PES measures could include reforestation payments made by cities to upstream communities in rural areas of shared watersheds for improved quantities and quality of fresh water for municipal users. Ecoservice payments by farmers to upstream forest stewards for properly managing the flow of soil nutrients, and methods to monetise the carbon sequestration and emission reduction credit benefits of green agriculture practices in order to compensate farmers for their efforts to restore and build SOM and employ other practices."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_alteration_and_biotechnology">Crop alteration and biotechnology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Crop alteration and biotechnology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">Plant breeding</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ueberladewagen_(jha).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/220px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/330px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/440px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1094" data-file-height="710" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ueberladewagen_(jha).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">Tractor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaser_bin" title="Chaser bin">chaser bin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Crop alteration has been practiced by humankind for thousands of years, since the beginning of civilization. Altering crops through breeding practices changes the genetic make-up of a plant to develop crops with more beneficial characteristics for humans, for example, larger fruits or seeds, drought-tolerance, or resistance to pests. Significant advances in plant breeding ensued after the work of geneticist <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a>. His work on <a href="/wiki/Dominant_allele" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominant allele">dominant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Recessive_allele" class="mw-redirect" title="Recessive allele">recessive alleles</a>, although initially largely ignored for almost 50 years, gave plant breeders a better understanding of genetics and breeding techniques. Crop breeding includes techniques such as plant selection with desirable traits, <a href="/wiki/Self-pollination" title="Self-pollination">self-pollination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-pollination" class="mw-redirect" title="Cross-pollination">cross-pollination</a>, and molecular techniques that genetically modify the organism.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Domestication of plants has, over the centuries increased yield, improved disease resistance and <a href="/wiki/Drought_tolerance" title="Drought tolerance">drought tolerance</a>, eased harvest and improved the taste and nutritional value of crop plants. Careful selection and breeding have had enormous effects on the characteristics of crop plants. Plant selection and breeding in the 1920s and 1930s improved pasture (grasses and clover) in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. Extensive X-ray and ultraviolet induced mutagenesis efforts (i.e. primitive genetic engineering) during the 1950s produced the modern commercial varieties of grains such as wheat, corn (maize) and barley.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> popularized the use of conventional <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> to sharply increase yield by creating "high-yielding varieties". For example, average yields of corn (maize) in the USA have increased from around 2.5 tons per hectare (t/ha) (40 bushels per acre) in 1900 to about 9.4 t/ha (150 bushels per acre) in 2001. Similarly, worldwide average wheat yields have increased from less than 1 t/ha in 1900 to more than 2.5 t/ha in 1990. South American average wheat yields are around 2 t/ha, African under 1 t/ha, and Egypt and Arabia up to 3.5 to 4 t/ha with irrigation. In contrast, the average wheat yield in countries such as France is over 8 t/ha. Variations in yields are due mainly to variation in climate, genetics, and the level of intensive farming techniques (use of fertilizers, chemical pest control, growth control to avoid lodging).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Genetic engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">Genetically modified food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_the_release_of_genetic_modified_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms">Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">Genetically modified food controversies</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organisms</a> (GMO) are <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> material has been altered by genetic engineering techniques generally known as <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Recombinant DNA technology">recombinant DNA technology</a>. Genetic engineering has expanded the genes available to breeders to utilize in creating desired germlines for new crops. Increased durability, nutritional content, insect and virus resistance and herbicide tolerance are a few of the attributes bred into crops through genetic engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> For some, GMO crops cause <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_labeling_regulations" class="mw-redirect" title="Food labeling regulations">food labeling</a> concerns. Numerous countries have placed restrictions on the production, import or use of GMO foods and crops, which have been put in place due to concerns over potential health issues, declining agricultural diversity and contamination of non-GMO crops.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Currently a global treaty, the <a href="/wiki/Biosafety_Protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Biosafety Protocol">Biosafety Protocol</a>, regulates the trade of GMOs. There is ongoing discussion regarding the labeling of foods made from GMOs, and while the EU currently requires all GMO foods to be labeled, the US does not.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Herbicide-resistant seed has a gene implanted into its genome that allows the plants to tolerate exposure to herbicides, including <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosates</a>. These seeds allow the farmer to grow a crop that can be sprayed with herbicides to control weeds without harming the resistant crop. Herbicide-tolerant crops are used by farmers worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> With the increasing use of herbicide-tolerant crops, comes an increase in the use of glyphosate-based herbicide sprays. In some areas glyphosate resistant weeds have developed, causing farmers to switch to other herbicides.<sup id="cite_ref-Farmers_Guide_to_GMOs_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farmers_Guide_to_GMOs-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Some studies also link widespread glyphosate usage to iron deficiencies in some crops, which is both a crop production and a nutritional quality concern, with potential economic and health implications.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other GMO crops used by growers include insect-resistant crops, which have a gene from the soil bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i> (Bt), which produces a toxin specific to insects. These crops protect plants from damage by insects.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Some believe that similar or better pest-resistance traits can be acquired through traditional breeding practices, and resistance to various pests can be gained through hybridization or cross-pollination with wild species. In some cases, wild species are the primary source of resistance traits; some tomato cultivars that have gained resistance to at least 19 diseases did so through crossing with wild populations of tomatoes.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_with_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental issues with agriculture">Environmental issues with agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/220px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/330px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/440px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">Water pollution</a> in a rural <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">stream</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_pollution_in_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Agricultural pollution in New Zealand">runoff from farming activity in New Zealand</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Agriculture, as implemented through the method of farming, imposes external costs upon society through pesticides, nutrient runoff, excessive water usage, loss of natural environment and assorted other problems. A 2000 assessment of agriculture in the UK determined total external costs for 1996 of £2,343 million, or £208 per hectare.<sup id="cite_ref-Pretty2000_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pretty2000-94">[94]</a></sup> A 2005 analysis of these costs in the USA concluded that cropland imposes approximately $5 to 16 billion ($30 to $96 per hectare), while livestock production imposes $714 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Tegtmeier2005_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tegtmeier2005-95">[95]</a></sup> Both studies, which focused solely on the fiscal impacts, concluded that more should be done to internalize external costs. Neither included subsidies in their analysis, but they noted that subsidies also influence the cost of agriculture to society.<sup id="cite_ref-Pretty2000_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pretty2000-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tegtmeier2005_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tegtmeier2005-95">[95]</a></sup> In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> published a report assessing the environmental impacts of consumption and production. The study found that agriculture and food consumption are two of the most important drivers of environmental pressures, particularly habitat change, climate change, water use and toxic emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The 2011 UNEP Green Economy report states that "[a]gricultural operations, excluding land use changes, produce approximately 13 per cent of anthropogenic global GHG emissions. This includes GHGs emitted by the use of inorganic fertilisers agro-chemical pesticides and herbicides; (GHG emissions resulting from production of these inputs are included in industrial emissions); and fossil fuel-energy inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup> "On average we find that the total amount of fresh residues from agricultural and forestry production for second- generation biofuel production amounts to 3.8 billion tonnes per year between 2011 and 2050 (with an average annual growth rate of 11 per cent throughout the period analysed, accounting for higher growth during early years, 48 per cent for 2011–2020 and an average 2 per cent annual expansion after 2020)."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_issues">Livestock issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Livestock issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A senior UN official and co-author of a UN report detailing this problem, Henning Steinfeld, said "Livestock are one of the most significant contributors to today's most serious environmental problems".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Livestock production occupies 70% of all land used for agriculture, or 30% of the land surface of the planet. It is one of the largest sources of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, responsible for 18% of the world's greenhouse gas emissions as measured in CO<sub>2</sub> equivalents. By comparison, all transportation emits 13.5% of the CO<sub>2</sub>. It produces 65% of human-related <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (which has 296 times the global warming potential of CO<sub>2,</sub>) and 37% of all human-induced <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (which is 23 times as warming as CO<sub>2</sub>.) It also generates 64% of the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> emission. Livestock expansion is cited as a key factor driving <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>; in the Amazon basin 70% of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_Rainforest" class="mw-redirect" title="Deforestation of the Amazon Rainforest">previously forested area</a> is now occupied by pastures and the remainder used for feedcrops.<sup id="cite_ref-LEAD_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LEAD-98">[98]</a></sup> Through deforestation and <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>, livestock is also driving reductions in biodiversity. Furthermore, the UNEP states that "methane emissions from global livestock are projected to increase by 60 per cent by 2030 under current practices and consumption patterns."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_and_water_issues">Land and water issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Land and water issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_irrigation" title="Environmental impact of irrigation">Environmental impact of irrigation</a></div>
<p>Land transformation, the use of land to yield goods and services, is the most substantial way humans alter the Earth's ecosystems, and is considered the driving force in the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">loss of biodiversity</a>. Estimates of the amount of land transformed by humans vary from 39 to 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-Vitousek_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vitousek-99">[99]</a></sup> Land degradation, the long-term decline in ecosystem function and productivity, is estimated to be occurring on 24% of land worldwide, with cropland overrepresented.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_GLADA_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_GLADA-100">[100]</a></sup> The UN-FAO report cites land management as the driving factor behind degradation and reports that 1.5 billion people rely upon the degrading land. Degradation can be deforestation, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, mineral depletion, or chemical degradation (<a href="/wiki/Soil_acidification" title="Soil acidification">acidification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salinization" class="mw-redirect" title="Salinization">salinization</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">Eutrophication</a>, excessive nutrients in <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic ecosystems</a> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">anoxia</a>, leads to <a href="/wiki/Fish_kill" title="Fish kill">fish kills</a>, loss of biodiversity, and renders water unfit for drinking and other industrial uses. Excessive fertilization and manure application to cropland, as well as high livestock stocking densities cause nutrient (mainly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>) <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(agriculture)" title="Leaching (agriculture)">leaching</a> from agricultural land. These nutrients are major <a href="/wiki/Nonpoint_source_pollution" title="Nonpoint source pollution">nonpoint pollutants</a> contributing to eutrophication of aquatic ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-Eutr_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eutr-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Agriculture accounts for 70 percent of withdrawals of freshwater resources.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Agriculture is a major draw on water from <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, and currently draws from those underground water sources at an unsustainable rate. It is long known that aquifers in areas as diverse as northern China, the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Upper Ganges</a> and the western US are being depleted, and new research extends these problems to aquifers in Iran, Mexico and Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Increasing pressure is being placed on water resources by industry and urban areas, meaning that <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> is increasing and agriculture is facing the challenge of producing more food for the world's growing population with reduced water resources.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Farm_water" title="Farm water">Agricultural water</a> usage can also cause major environmental problems, including the destruction of natural wetlands, the spread of water-borne diseases, and land degradation through salinization and waterlogging, when irrigation is performed incorrectly.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pesticides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_pesticides" title="Environmental impact of pesticides">Environmental impact of pesticides</a></div>
<p>Pesticide use has increased since 1950 to 2.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million short tons annually worldwide, yet crop loss from pests has remained relatively constant.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimentel_pesticide_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimentel_pesticide-106">[106]</a></sup> The World Health Organization estimated in 1992 that 3<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million pesticide poisonings occur annually, causing 220,000 deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-107">[107]</a></sup> Pesticides select for <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_resistance" title="Pesticide resistance">pesticide resistance</a> in the pest population, leading to a condition termed the "pesticide treadmill" in which pest resistance warrants the development of a new pesticide.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_Pest_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS_Pest-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternative argument is that the way to "save the environment" and prevent famine is by using pesticides and intensive high yield farming, a view exemplified by a quote heading the Center for Global Food Issues website: 'Growing more per acre leaves more land for nature'.<sup id="cite_ref-DAvery_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DAvery-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> However, critics argue that a trade-off between the environment and a need for food is not inevitable,<sup id="cite_ref-WH_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH-111">[111]</a></sup> and that pesticides simply replace <a href="/wiki/Good_agricultural_practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Good agricultural practices">good agronomic practices</a> such as crop rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_Pest_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS_Pest-108">[108]</a></sup> The UNEP introduces the <a href="/wiki/Push%E2%80%93pull_agricultural_pest_management" title="Push–pull agricultural pest management">Push–pull agricultural pest management</a> technique which involves intercropping that uses plant aromas to repel or push away pests while pulling in or attracting the right insects. "The implementation of push-pull in eastern Africa has significantly increased maize yields and the combined cultivation of N-fixing forage crops has enriched the soil and has also provided farmers with feed for livestock. With increased livestock operations, the farmers are able to produce meat, milk and other dairy products and they use the manure as organic fertiliser that returns nutrients to the fields."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">Climate change and agriculture</a></div>
<p>Climate change has the potential to affect agriculture through changes in temperature, rainfall (timing and quantity), <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> and the interaction of these elements.<sup id="cite_ref-CS_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CS-62">[62]</a></sup> Extreme events, such as droughts and floods, are forecast to increase as climate change takes hold.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Agriculture is among sectors most vulnerable to the impacts of climate change; water supply for example, will be critical to sustain agricultural production and provide the increase in food output required to sustain the world's growing population. Fluctuations in the flow of rivers are likely to increase in the twenty-first century. Based on the experience of countries in the Nile river basin (Ethiopia, Kenya and Sudan) and other developing countries, depletion of water resources during seasons crucial for agriculture can lead to a decline in yield by up to 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Transformational approaches will be needed to manage natural resources in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> For example, policies, practices and tools promoting climate-smart agriculture will be important, as will better use of scientific information on climate for assessing risks and vulnerability. Planners and policy-makers will need to help create suitable policies that encourage funding for such agricultural transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Agriculture in its many forms can both mitigate or worsen <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Some of the increase in CO<sub>2</sub> in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> in the soil, and much of the methane emitted into the atmosphere is caused by the decomposition of organic matter in wet soils such as rice <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddy fields</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Soils_OM_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soils_OM-116">[116]</a></sup> as well as the normal digestive activities of farm animals. Further, wet or <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">anaerobic</a> soils also lose nitrogen through <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">denitrification</a>, releasing the greenhouse gases <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> and nitrous oxide.<sup id="cite_ref-Soils_N_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soils_N-117">[117]</a></sup> Changes in management can reduce the release of these greenhouse gases, and soil can further be used to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">sequester</a> some of the CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-Soils_OM_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soils_OM-116">[116]</a></sup> Informed by the UNEP, "[a]griculture also produces about 58 per cent of global nitrous oxide emissions and about 47 per cent of global methane emissions. Cattle and rice farms release methane, fertilized fields release nitrous oxide, and the cutting down of rainforests to grow crops or raise livestock releases carbon dioxide.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Both of these gases have a far greater global warming potential per tonne than CO2 (298 times and 25 times respectively)."<sup id="cite_ref-unep.org_78-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are several factors within the field of agriculture that contribute to the large amount of CO2 emissions. The diversity of the sources ranges from the production of farming tools to the transport of harvested produce. Approximately 8% of the national carbon footprint is due to agricultural sources. Of that, 75% is of the carbon emissions released from the production of crop assisting chemicals.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Factories producing insecticides, herbicides, fungicides, and fertilizers are a major culprit of the greenhouse gas. Productivity on the farm itself and the use of machinery is another source of the carbon emission. Almost all the industrial machines used in modern farming are powered by fossil fuels. These instruments are burning fossil fuels from the beginning of the process to the end. Tractors are the root of this source. The tractor is going to burn fuel and release CO2 just to run. The amount of emissions from the machinery increase with the attachment of different units and need for more power. During the soil preparation stage tillers and plows will be used to disrupt the soil. During growth watering pumps and sprayers are used to keep the crops hydrated. And when the crops are ready for picking a forage or combine harvester is used. These types of machinery all require additional energy which leads to increased carbon dioxide emissions from the basic tractors.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The final major contribution to CO2 emissions in agriculture is in the final transport of produce. Local farming suffered a decline over the past century due to large amounts of farm subsidies. The majority of crops are shipped hundreds of miles to various processing plants before ending up in the grocery store. These shipments are made using fossil fuel burning modes of transportation. Inevitably these transport adds to carbon dioxide emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sustainable_agriculture_topics" class="mw-redirect" title="List of sustainable agriculture topics">List of sustainable agriculture topics</a></div>
<p>Some major organizations are hailing farming within <a href="/wiki/Agroecosystem" title="Agroecosystem">agroecosystems</a> as the way forward for mainstream agriculture. Current farming methods have resulted in over-stretched water resources, high levels of erosion and reduced soil fertility. According to a report by the <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Management_Institute" title="International Water Management Institute">International Water Management Institute</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNEP" class="mw-redirect" title="UNEP">UNEP</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> there is not enough water to continue farming using current practices; therefore how critical water, land, and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> resources are used to boost crop yields must be reconsidered. The report suggested assigning value to ecosystems, recognizing environmental and livelihood tradeoffs, and balancing the rights of a variety of users and interests. Inequities that result when such measures are adopted would need to be addressed, such as the reallocation of water from poor to rich, the clearing of land to make way for more productive farmland, or the preservation of a wetland system that limits fishing rights.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Technological advancements help provide farmers with tools and resources to make farming more sustainable.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> New technologies have given rise to innovations like <a href="/wiki/Conservation_tillage" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation tillage">conservation tillage</a>, a farming process which helps prevent land loss to erosion, water pollution and enhances carbon sequestration.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a report by the International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI),<sup id="cite_ref-ifpri_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ifpri-77">[77]</a></sup> agricultural technologies will have the greatest impact on food production if adopted in combination with each other; using a model that assessed how eleven technologies could impact agricultural productivity, food security and trade by 2050, IFPRI found that the number of people at risk from hunger could be reduced by as much as 40% and food prices could be reduced by almost half.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_economics">Agricultural economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Agricultural economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_economics" title="Agricultural economics">Agricultural economics</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">Agricultural subsidy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rural_economics" title="Rural economics">Rural economics</a></div>
<p>Agricultural economics refers to economics as it relates to the "production, distribution and consumption of [agricultural] goods and services".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Combining agricultural production with general theories of marketing and business as a discipline of study began in the late 1800s, and grew significantly through the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Although the study of agricultural economics is relatively recent, major trends in agriculture have significantly affected national and international economies throughout history, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Tenant_farmer" title="Tenant farmer">tenant farmers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharecropping" title="Sharecropping">sharecropping</a> in the post-<a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> to the European <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> system of <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In the United States, and elsewhere, food costs attributed to <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a>, distribution, and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_marketing" title="Agricultural marketing">agricultural marketing</a>, sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_value_chain" title="Agricultural value chain">value chain</a>, have risen while the costs attributed to farming have declined. This is related to the greater efficiency of farming, combined with the increased level of <a href="/wiki/Value_added" title="Value added">value addition</a> (e.g. more highly processed products) provided by the supply chain. <a href="/wiki/Market_concentration" title="Market concentration">Market concentration</a> has increased in the sector as well, and although the total effect of the increased market concentration is likely increased efficiency, the changes redistribute <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">economic surplus</a> from producers (farmers) and consumers, and may have negative implications for rural communities.<sup id="cite_ref-Sexton2000_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sexton2000-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>National government policies can significantly change the economic marketplace for agricultural products, in the form of taxation, <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">subsidies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> and other measures.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Since at least the 1960s, a combination of import/export restrictions, <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate_policy" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange rate policy">exchange rate policies</a> and subsidies have affected farmers in both the developing and developed world. In the 1980s, it was clear that non-subsidized farmers in developing countries were experiencing adverse effects from national policies that created artificially low global prices for farm products. Between the mid-1980s and the early 2000s, several international agreements were put into place that limited agricultural tariffs, subsidies and other trade restrictions.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, as of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there was still a significant amount of policy-driven distortion in global agricultural product prices. The three agricultural products with the greatest amount of trade distortion were sugar, milk and rice, mainly due to taxation. Among the <a href="/wiki/Oilseed" class="mw-redirect" title="Oilseed">oilseeds</a>, sesame had the greatest amount of taxation, but overall, feed grains and oilseeds had much lower levels of taxation than livestock products. Since the 1980s, policy-driven distortions have seen a greater decrease among livestock products than crops during the worldwide reforms in agricultural policy.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Despite this progress, certain crops, such as cotton, still see subsidies in developed countries artificially deflating global prices, causing hardship in developing countries with non-subsidized farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Unprocessed commodities (i.e. corn, soybeans, cows) are generally graded to indicate quality. The quality affects the price the producer receives. Commodities are generally reported by production quantities, such as volume, number or weight.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_science">Agricultural science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Agricultural science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">Agricultural science</a></div>
<p><b>Agricultural science</b> is a broad multidisciplinary field of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> that encompasses the parts of exact, natural, economic and <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> that are used in the practice and understanding of <strong class="selflink">agriculture</strong>. (<a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">Veterinary science</a>, but not <a href="/wiki/Animal_science" title="Animal science">animal science</a>, is often excluded from the definition.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_countries_by_agricultural_output">List of countries by agricultural output</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: List of countries by agricultural output">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_agriculture">Energy and agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Energy and agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since the 1940s, agricultural productivity has increased dramatically, due largely to the increased use of energy-intensive mechanization, fertilizers and pesticides. The vast majority of this energy input comes from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> sources.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Between the 1960–65 measuring cycle and the cycle from 1986 to 1990, the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the globe, with world grain production increasing significantly (between 70% and 390% for wheat and 60% to 150% for rice, depending on geographic area)<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> doubled. Modern agriculture's heavy reliance on <a href="/wiki/Petrochemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrochemicals">petrochemicals</a> and mechanization has raised concerns that oil shortages could increase costs and reduce agricultural output, causing food shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; text-align:center;">
<tr>
<th colspan="4">Agriculture and food system share (%) of total energy<br />
consumption by three industrialized nations</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Country</th>
<th>Year</th>
<th>Agriculture<br />
(direct &amp; indirect)</th>
<th>Food<br />
system</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left;">United Kingdom<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></td>
<td>2005</td>
<td>1.9</td>
<td>11</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left;">United States<sup id="cite_ref-ers.usda.gov_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ers.usda.gov-140">[140]</a></sup></td>
<td>2002</td>
<td>2.0</td>
<td>14</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left;">Sweden<sup id="cite_ref-WallgrenH.C3.B6jer2009_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WallgrenH.C3.B6jer2009-141">[141]</a></sup></td>
<td>2000</td>
<td>2.5</td>
<td>13</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Modern or industrialized agriculture is dependent on fossil fuels in two fundamental ways: 1. direct consumption on the farm and 2. indirect consumption to manufacture inputs used on the farm. Direct consumption includes the use of lubricants and fuels to operate farm vehicles and machinery; and use of gasoline, liquid <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, and electricity to power dryers, pumps, lights, heaters, and coolers. American farms directly consumed about 1.2 exajoules (1.1 quadrillion BTU) in 2002, or just over 1% of the nation's total energy.<sup id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indirect consumption is mainly oil and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> used to manufacture fertilizers and pesticides, which accounted for 0.6 exajoules (0.6 quadrillion BTU) in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-138">[138]</a></sup> The natural gas and coal consumed by the production of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fertilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogen fertilizer">nitrogen fertilizer</a> can account for over half of the agricultural energy usage. China utilizes mostly coal in the production of nitrogen fertilizer, while most of Europe uses large amounts of natural gas and small amounts of coal. According to a 2010 report published by <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="The Royal Society">The Royal Society</a>, agriculture is increasingly dependent on the direct and indirect input of fossil fuels. Overall, the fuels used in agriculture vary based on several factors, including crop, production system and location.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The energy used to manufacture farm machinery is also a form of indirect agricultural energy consumption. Together, direct and indirect consumption by US farms accounts for about 2% of the nation's energy use. Direct and indirect energy consumption by U.S. farms peaked in 1979, and has gradually declined over the past 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-138">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Food_systems" title="Food systems">Food systems</a> encompass not just agricultural production, but also off-farm processing, packaging, transporting, marketing, consumption, and disposal of food and food-related items. Agriculture accounts for less than one-fifth of food system energy use in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-css.snre.umich.edu_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-css.snre.umich.edu-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ers.usda.gov_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ers.usda.gov-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitigation_of_effects_of_petroleum_shortages"><span id="Agriculture_and_petroleum"></span><span id="Mitigation_of_effects_of_petroleum_shortages">Mitigation of effects of petroleum shortages</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mitigation of effects of petroleum shortages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/220px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/330px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/440px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert's</a> prediction of world petroleum production rates. Modern agriculture is totally reliant on petroleum energy<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the event of a petroleum shortage (see <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a> for global concerns), organic agriculture can be more attractive than conventional practices that use petroleum-based pesticides, herbicides, or fertilizers. Some studies using modern organic-farming methods have reported yields equal to or higher than those available from conventional farming.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Soviet Union">fall of the Soviet Union</a>, with shortages of conventional petroleum-based inputs, Cuba made use of mostly organic practices, including <a href="/wiki/Biopesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Biopesticides">biopesticides</a>, plant-based pesticides and sustainable cropping practices, to feed its populace.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> However, organic farming may be more <a href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour (economics)">labor</a>-intensive and would require a shift of the workforce from urban to rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Soil_conditioner" title="Soil conditioner">reconditioning of soil</a> to restore organic matter lost during the use of monoculture agriculture techniques is important to provide a reservoir of plant-available nutrients, to maintain texture, and to minimize erosion.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that rural communities might obtain fuel from the <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synfuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Synfuel">synfuel</a> process, which uses agricultural <i>waste</i> to provide charcoal fertilizer, some fuel <i>and</i> food, instead of the normal <a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">food vs. fuel</a> debate. As the synfuel would be used on-site, the process would be more efficient and might just provide enough fuel for a new organic-agriculture fusion.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that some transgenic plants may some day be developed which would allow for maintaining or increasing yields while requiring fewer fossil-fuel-derived inputs than conventional crops.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The possibility of success of these programs is questioned by ecologists and economists concerned with unsustainable GMO practices such as <a href="/wiki/Terminator_seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Terminator seeds">terminator seeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Radel_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radel-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> While there has been some research on sustainability using GMO crops, at least one prominent multi-year attempt by <a href="/wiki/Monsanto_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Monsanto Company">Monsanto Company</a> has been unsuccessful, though during the same period traditional breeding techniques yielded a more sustainable variety of the same crop.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy">Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_farm_subsidies_(source_Congressional_Budget_Office).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg/290px-United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg.png" width="290" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg/435px-United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg/580px-United_States_farm_subsidies_%28source_Congressional_Budget_Office%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="540" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_farm_subsidies_(source_Congressional_Budget_Office).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">Congressional Budget Office</a> report</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a> is the set of government decisions and actions relating to domestic agriculture and imports of foreign agricultural products. Governments usually implement agricultural policies with the goal of achieving a specific outcome in the domestic agricultural product markets. Some overarching themes include risk management and adjustment (including policies related to climate change, food safety and natural disasters), <a href="/wiki/Economic_stability" title="Economic stability">economic stability</a> (including policies related to taxes), natural resources and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_sustainability" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental sustainability">environmental sustainability</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Water_resource_management" title="Water resource management">water policy</a>), research and development, and market access for domestic commodities (including relations with global organizations and agreements with other countries).<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Agricultural policy can also touch on <a href="/wiki/Food_quality" title="Food quality">food quality</a>, ensuring that the food supply is of a consistent and known quality, food security, ensuring that the food supply meets the population's needs, and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a>. Policy programs can range from financial programs, such as subsidies, to encouraging producers to enroll in voluntary quality assurance programs.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are many influences on the creation of agricultural policy, including consumers, agribusiness, trade lobbies and other groups. <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">Agribusiness</a> interests hold a large amount of influence over policy making, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Campaign_contribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Campaign contribution">campaign contributions</a>. Political action groups, including those interested in environmental issues and <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a>, also provide influence, as do lobbying organizations representing individual agricultural commodities.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization_of_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations">Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations</a> (FAO) leads international efforts to defeat hunger and provides a forum for the negotiation of global agricultural regulations and agreements. Dr. Samuel Jutzi, director of FAO's animal production and health division, states that lobbying by large corporations has stopped reforms that would improve human health and the environment. For example, proposals in 2010 for a voluntary code of conduct for the livestock industry that would have provided incentives for improving standards for health, and environmental regulations, such as the number of animals an area of land can support without long-term damage, were successfully defeated due to large food company pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
